		<date>2011-12-24</date>
		<username>Paul Andrews (poolandrews@hotmail.com)</username>
		<score>0.6</score>
		<shortDescription>Surprisingly quite suspenseful &amp; good US remake.</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Quarantine starts as a reporter named Angela Vidal (Jennifer Carpenter) for the television show 'Night Shift' is assigned to spend a night with the Los Angeles fire department as part of a story, along with her cameraman Scott Percival (Steve Harris) they are paired up with firefighters Jake (Jay Hernandez) &amp; George (Johnathon Schaech) who are called out to an emergency. Angela &amp; Scott shadow them to a four storey apartment block where strange noises have been heard in one of the apartments belonging to an elderly woman named Espinoza, breaking the door down the fire &amp; police crews discover her standing there, blood stained &amp; frothing at the mouth. Espinoza attacks one of the cops &amp; bites his neck, in a panic the other's try to leave the building for proper help but find that the authorities outside have seal all exits &amp; declared the apartment block a quarantine zone. Those still trapped inside soon find out that a mutant strain of rabies is turning people in flesh-eating zombies...Written &amp; directed by brother's John Erick Dowdle &amp; Drew Dowdle this is the US remake of a Spanish film called Rec (2007) which seems to have a lot of fans whom seem to dislike this remake, judging by a lot of the negative comments &amp; reviews this dislike seems to stem almost entirely from the fact that Quarantine is a remake without acknowledging anything else. I will admit straight away that I have never seen Rec so cannot compare the two, maybe because I haven't seen it means I have no expectation whatsoever &amp; therefore ended up enjoying Quarantine substantially more than I expected to. I think the script is pretty good, there's a little bit of humour &amp; character development at the start before the main story kicks in but once it does kick in the script is fairly blunt with people forgetting things like manners or reciting some lengthy back-story which is actually pretty believable in the situation. I mean if you found yourself trapped inside an infected building would you waste time telling some distant insignificant story from your past? Would you really care if no-one else there liked you? The script gives us just about enough information to care when all hell starts to break loose, people start arguing &amp; the relationship's start to fall apart. There's clichéd romance, no happy ending for anyone &amp; just about enough ambiguity to keep things mysterious with just enough pieces of the puzzle revealed for the audience to fit together &amp; make sense of what's going on. At just under 90 minutes in length the found footage nature of the narrative is nicely done &amp; it moves along at a good pace with the film becoming more frantic &amp; darker as it progresses, Quarantine is a sort of mix of The Blair Witch Project (1999) &amp; 28 Days Later... (2002) in both story &amp; style.Despite not usually liking the shaky hand-held camcorder way of shooting a film I thought it worked well in Quarantine, even though it can get a bit shaky &amp; blurred at times I always knew what was going on unlike a lot of films which mix shaky camera footage &amp; fast editing which is a real no no in my book as it just ends up annoying. The attack scenes are quick &amp; effective, there's some decent gore here with a splattered Rat, various bites &amp; gushing blood, a drill in someone's head &amp; a guy trying to walk on a broken leg with his bones sticking out of the flesh in what must have been very painful. I felt the director did a great job in building up the tension &amp; suspense here, the shaky camera-work also gets across the sort of panic, confusion &amp; fear that would have been present.With a supposed budget of about $12,000,000 this looks fine with good production values &amp; effects, unusually there is no musical score at all. Largely filmed in Los Angeles in California. Production company Verrtigo Films seem to specialise in US remakes of foreign horror with films like The Grudge (2004), Dark Water (2005), The Departed (2006), The Eye (2008), Shutter (2008), The Echo (2008) &amp; The Uninvited (2009). The acting is pretty strong here, Jennifer Carpenter is not only nice to look at but she is quite good as she slowly loses it &amp; by the end becomes a complete wreck.Quarantine turned out a whole lot better than I had expected, it's a pretty effective little scare film that I thought delivered the goods. Not a masterpiece but a good solid horror film anyway, followed by Quarantine 2: Terminal (2011).</fullReview>
	</review>

		<date>2010-10-25</date>
		<username>muvcritic</username>
		<score>0.7</score>
		<shortDescription>Zombies Not So Slow in Quarantine</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>(originally appeared in Free Times, Columbia's entertainment guide)Even though I'm fascinated by the "living dead" genre as a metaphor for the end of civilization -- which, judging by the financial crisis, is next week -- there's too many of the things. What can they say that we haven't already heard? Quarantine takes an idea that's been beat to undeath and does a damn scary job of it, emulating the documentary "you are there" feel of Cloverfield, the same approach as last year's Diary of the Dead, by zombie inventor George Romero. Sorry, George, but Quarantine is the better cinematic treatment, in that it's less self-conscious. George's characters, film students, wax poetic about the relationship between violence and film, whereas director John Erick Dowdle's characters are too busy running from zombies to intellectualize. Or are they? Zombies, I mean.Technically, the creatures in Quarantine aren't actually dead, but if it has a glassy stare like a zombie, moans like a zombie, eats the flesh of the living like a zombie, in my book, it's a zombie. Dowdle's in the same denial about his film being a living dead movie as Danny Boyle when he made 28 Days Later.But Dowdle hooked me with a brilliant set-up: A newslady played by Jennifer Carpenter (The Exorcism of Emily Rose), shooting a feature story about the fire department, playfully flirts with the firefighters as she tours their station. I've shot half a dozen TV stories just like it, and everything rings so authentic about an unrehearsed location shoot that it's a perfect lead-in to the terrifying events that follow, as she accompanies them on what they believe to be a routine medical call. When they get to a downtown apartment building, Dowdle stages an elaborate introductory sequence, which appears to be a single take, that rapidly demonstrates that the call is anything but routine. The chilling unpredictability coupled with the nauseating "bounce" of the hand-held camera renders Quarantine mighty scary.There's only one thing I don't like, and READ NO FURTHER if you don't want to know. It has nothing to do with the film; it's the trailer, which contains a shot that I thought when I saw it, "That couldn't be the last shot in the movie...could it?" Unbelievably, it could. Dowdle ought to be livid at the marketing flaks who thought it was a brilliant idea to telegraph the ending of a suspense thriller.If you can ignore that, which is an awfully tall order, Quarantine is a supremely creepy Halloween entry that examines, in microcosm, how Americans react in a disaster. It's not encouraging, but it's a well-directed film.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<fullReview>Ever since the "Blair Witch Project" first came out, the use of a first person perspective has become wildly popular in horror movies over the years. "Quarantine" is no different. A remake of the Spanish film, "Rec", the movie follows a group of people trapped inside an L.A. apartment building trying to survive a very contagious, mutated form of rabies that starts to infect people one by one. The film does have a lot going for it. The actors, especially Jennifer Carpenter, are all very good and keep the viewer entertained, even though most of what they do is running and screaming. The infected (basically fast zombies) are also quite frightening and believable as rabies and it's symptoms are very real. There's also enough action and blood to satisfy any horror fan. Some of the death scenes in particular are really cool (my personal favorite involves using the camera itself to kill a zombie). My only real beef with this film is it's first ten minutes of buildup. It's very boring and could've definitely been shortened. Overall, while it may be seen as kind of lazy to remake a movie that came out in 2007, there's enough original twists and perks in it to have it stand out. This is definitely worth a watch.</fullReview>

		<date>2010-07-23</date>
		<username>MBunge</username>
		<score>0.7</score>
		<shortDescription>A modern must-see for horror fans</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>A remake of a Spanish horror film, Quarantine is gripping, claustrophobic and scary as all get out. It's also one of the few movies of its kind that won't make you vomit?but I'll get to that later.Angela Videll (Jennifer Carpenter) is a pretty young reporter who works for a Los Angeles-based infotainment show called "The Night Shift". While doing a story about a life at a fire house one night, Angela and her cameraman Scott Percival get to go along on an emergency call. Trailing after two firefighters named Jake and Fletcher (Jay Hernandez and Johnathon Schaech), they arrive at an old, four-story apartment building to check on reports of a screaming old woman. Joined by a pair of cops, Angela, Scott, Jake and Fletcher head up to the old woman's apartment. They find the old woman foaming at the mouth and with blood on her nightgown that doesn't appear to be hers. They try to help here, but then the old woman attacks one of the cops and bites off a hunk of flesh from his neck. There's something in the building that's changing people into animalistic monsters. But when Angela and company try to flee, they discover something even more terrifying. The building has been surrounded by soldiers and they won't let anyone get out.This is one of the better horror movies that I've seen, largely because it manages to be frightening and shocking on more than one level. There's the thrilling fear of having to dodge crazed cannibals mixed with the anxiety of the unknown, the dread of impending doom and the panic of helplessness. In some ways, when these characters realize they're being abandoned to their fate by their own government, that's even worse than what's trying to eat them alive.Now, Quarantine may have a great concept and be very well-executed, but it's still a fairly straight forward horror flick. So, there's not exactly a lot of brilliant dialog or characterization on display here. The stuff with Angela and the firefighters before they get the emergency call, however, is better than the usual set up these kind of films have. Like most really good horror movies, it doesn't act like a horror movie until people start getting chomped on. It also explains what's happening to the audience with a minimum of clunky exposition and does a great job of slowly and believably ratcheting up the fear and danger until it reaches near-hysteria at the story's climax.And about that non-vomiting business? Quarantine is shot real-video style like The Blair Witch Project or Cloverfield. Everything we see is through the lens of Scott, Angela's cameraman. Unlike most real-video movies, I was able to get through this one without feeling a hint of motion sickness. Because the conceit of the movie is that it's being shot by a professional cameraman, there's a lot less of that shaky, jerky camera movement that makes some folks want to hurl. It still looks like real-video footage, but not like it was filmed by a drunk guy at a wedding in Afghanistan. So, if you want to experience real-video storytelling without having to worry about the old Technicolor yawn, Quarantine is the movie you've been waiting for. Director John Erick Dowdle also manages to do some things with this style of filmmaking that you probably haven't seen before, using the camera's point of view to maximize the shock and horror before your eyes.Quarantine is a compelling story told very well. It's one of the few modern horror films that all fans of the genre ought to see. And even if you're not necessarily a big horror fan, this movie is just plain good enough to entertain anybody.</fullReview>
	</review>

It was true horror and not that nonsense we see in a lot of the horror films that come out nowadays that show how much of a stupid society we are becoming as Americans.I was not looking forward to Quarantine because of a fear of mine: This is a remake, and it looks like REC, but in English. 		<date>2008-12-02</date>
		<username>DICK STEEL</username>
		<score>0.5</score>
		<shortDescription>A Nutshell Review: Quarantine</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Call it a case of deliberately engineered deja-vu, that I consciously decided to sit through this American remake of the recently screened Spanish horror film REC. Well of course the purpose is to discover if what's essentially the same story and technique employed, could get screwed up in a remake, given that Hollywood more often than not, lets down the original brilliance which they think they can replicate easily to translate to box office dollars.So what's the verdict? If you've already watched REC, then this one just simply pales in comparison, because of the need to be verbatim, and somehow the filmmakers here found it necessary to tell you every now and then, that there's actually someone behind the camera. Scott the cameraman (Steve Harris) appears quite often in front of the camera, well, and actually does so from the onset. Also, to up the body count quotient, new characters were included (although supporting ones), and unlike the original which had an opening scene dedicated to the fire station filled with more meat, here it drones on and on at how the assigned firemen are getting the hots for reporter Angela Vidal (Jennifer Carpenter).The storyline's almost the same, save for a few more extra scenes included to jazz up the gore department, and to incorporate some additional action elements. Otherwise, there's plenty of overacting here by everyone, so much so that instead of rooting for the characters to get out of their predicament, you're more likely to find yourself wishing that they meet their demise soon enough. Such is the original essence lost, and with that, what primarily works in REC.Between the two Angela Vidals, I'd confess that Jennifer Carpenter can't hold a candle to Manuela Velasco. The latter's the sweet damsel you'll risk life and limb to save, whereas the former, well, you'd just wish that she shuts the hell up and stop barking orders. I guess the direction of John Erick Dowdle will have take responsibility for that not-too-subtle difference between the Vidals. And through this film I'd learnt to appreciate how the shaky-cam was used to great effect in REC, and how easy it is to lapse into pitfalls as in Quarantine. Same storyline and plot development, same technique, relatively the same type of characters, but totally different outcomes in terms of how it engages the audience, because there were crucial moments where it failed to understand the need for restraint.Sure you can't expect rocket science to be coming out of this film, but unfortunately it joins the pile of "remakes that don't make it". Word is REC2 is currently in production, so I'll be looking forward to that, and hoping that there won't be a Quarantine 2 to embarrass itself.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<date>2008-10-30</date>
		<username>artemisfade</username>
		<score>0.7</score>
		<shortDescription>Surprisingly entertaining</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Quarantine is Hollywood's latest offering of the 'found film' format of horror/suspense, introduced to American audiences by The Blair Witch Project in 1999 and updated with this year's moderately successful Cloverfield, wherein the audience sees only what the character sees through the lens of his own hand-held camera. Based on the highly regarded Spanish film REC of last year, Quarantine is the story of a TV news reporter who finds herself covering a story a lot more horrifying than she anticipated.The film gets off to a slow start, taking its time to introduce the female protagonist, played by Jennifer Carpenter (TV's Dexter), as well as the firefighters she's supposed to be shadowing, including the likable Jay Hernandez (Hostel). While not doing much to endear the viewer to the characters, the opening scenes at least put the audience at ease before plunging everyone headfirst into non-stop zombie mayhem. And not George Romero's shambling undead, either; this film features dozens of sprinting, bloodthirsty, virus-infected creatures popularized by the Dawn of the Dead remake and 28 Days Later.Some of the scares are a little predictable, and the camera-work is needlessly shaky--much like Cloverfield--especially for a supposedly professional camera operator. However, the last half of the movie features pacing that keeps you on the edge of your seat even when the characters find a brief respite from the bloody carnage, and the story provides adequate explanation as to why our heroine runs upstairs instead of out of the zombie-infested apartment building.The acting is decent, and the visual and sound effects are brutal and believable. If you're the kind of person who takes pleasure in watching through the lens of a camera as it is used to beat a zombified woman to death, then this film is a must-see. That said, if you're one of the folks who got motion sickness from watching Cloverfield or the Bourne sequels, you might want to give this one a pass.</fullReview>
	</review>
	<review>
		<date>2008-10-20</date>
		<username>zardoz-13</username>
		<score>0.5</score>
		<shortDescription>Hair-Raising Hokum That Hath Little Horror!</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Imagine "Cloverfield" taking place in a creepy apartment complex with residents trying to elude zombies rather than in New York City as a "Godzilla" monster tries to stomp them, and you'll have the gist of "Full Moon Rising" director John Erick Dowdle's "Quarantine." Like "Cloverfield," "Quarantine" tells its story from the perspective of a television news videographer. The entire movie is literally seen through the camera viewfinder. This tense, eerie, sometimes exciting epic will only frighten those who have never seen a good zombie movie. What could have been a surefire horror classic is marred by a hammy lead actress and the marginal originality of writer &amp; director John Erick Dowdle and co-scenarist Drew Dowdle's screenplay. The Brothers Dowdle have remade the formulaic Spanish horror thriller "REC" (2007) that co-directors Jaume Balaguero and Paco Plaza wrote with Luis A. Berdejo. In "REC," a reality TV crew shadows fire department personnel as they fight to free a woman holed up in her apartment. The Dowdle Brothers have taken this bare-bones Spanish supernatural saga and added more zest to their flesh-eating disease chiller, but their low-budget and lack of imagination undermine them in the long run.The Dowdles do a solid job of setting up the premise. Budding TV journalist Angela Vidal (Jennifer Carpenter of "The Exorcism of Emily Rose") and her cameraman Scott (Steve Harris of "Diary of a Mad Black Woman) cover the night shift at a Los Angeles Fire Department station. While they await an inevitable 911 call, fellow firefighters Jake (Jay Hernandez of "Hostel") and George (Johnathon Schaech of "The Doom Generation") bond with the heroine and show her the ropes in what amounts to a variation on the John Travolta movie "Ladder 49." Giddy Angela gets to slide down the fire pole while her poor cameraman records her escapades. These two carefree firefighters compete each other to see who can get Angela in the sack first. Alas, they have forgotten that they are still wearing their microphones from an earlier interview and she can hear them. About a half-an-hour into the action, our heroes get more than they could have imagined in their worst nightmare when they respond to an alarm at an ordinary downtown apartment house. Arriving at the scene, the LAPD accompany our firefighters into the apartment complex and discover an older woman bathed in blood that doesn't look too congenial at first sight. Before our heroes know it, she takes a bite out of them.Later, we learn that one of the residents had taken his infected dog to a veterinarian and the doctor diagnosed a mysterious virus in the canine's system. The LAPD and a crew from the Center for Disease Control surround and seal off the apartment complex. A heavily armed S.W.A.T. team brandishes their assault rifles and threatens to shoot anybody that sets foot outside until their own haz-mat personnel can get inside to examine the bodies. Meanwhile, the firefighters and our heroine struggle to find a way out. Before long they learn from a television broadcast that they don't stand a chance in Hades of getting out alive. Initially, residents who appeared mildly sick begin to mutate into zombies, and our heroine and her cameraman record everything as evidence.Sadly, "Quarantine" suffers from several problems. First, the flighty TV journalist displays an obnoxious penchant for screaming at the least provocation. Furthermore, none of the characters emerge as remotely sympathetic. They are just victims. Second, the Dowdles rely on the first-person point of view of the cameraman to generate thrills and chills. At one point, Scott uses his camera to bludgeon a zombie. Even the other dogs in the building turn into flesh-eaters. However, the filmmakers do nothing with these mutant zombies that horror movie fanatics haven't seen in other films. Third, the authorities don't know how to kill zombies. Fourth, since the zombies never die, they multiply rapidly and munch on the residents as if they were Lays potato chips. For example, a uniformed LAPD officer pours a hail of bullets into the old Hispanic woman and the impact knocks her down. Nevertheless, he doesn't blow her head off, so she gets back up later and keeps on gnawing. Every zombie movie fanatic knows that the only way to kill a zombie is to shoot the sucker in the head. Mind you, the panicky camera work and the claustrophobic apartment complex with its shadowy premises will keep some people poised on the edge of their seats. "Quarantine" knows how to set-up a scary scene, but the pay-off is too predictable to make any of this exercise in helter-skelter chaos seem chilling. If you've seen the trailer, you've seen the best part of this nail-biter with an ending lifted from "Silence of the Lambs.""Quarantine," if it is any consolation, is a hundred times better than "The Zombie Diaries."</fullReview>
	</review>

I'd have to go back and re-watch REC, but I could swear it's a shot-for-shot remake. I'm pretty sure even the building used for the set is the same building. 		<shortDescription>Just as good as 'Rec', if not better</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>I honestly never imagined that I was going to want to defend this movie. After watching both Rec and Rec 2 yesterday and loving them, I had to see how badly the US had ruined yet another good original with "Quarantine". I myself hate the thought of remaking already excellent movies. But it's completely forgivable if you do it right. And Quarantine does it justice.I think the real story behind negative reviews for this movie is the simple nature of it being a remake. No one was trying to take anything from the Spanish people with this remake. They gave credit, and credit was certainly due. All this movie does is take the story to the next level. Just a Spanish audience isn't fair to such a powerful horror movie. And we all know that no one wants to sit and try to read subtitles while we're trying to enjoy a movie, and English dubs just don't cut it.Quarantine takes Rec and gives it an even greater feel, in my opinion. Everyone in the film's acting was much up to par with a great cast. Effects are of course far greater, which some think takes away from the realism. For me it didn't at all. The infected people were a lot more believable. When I'm watching a "movie" I don't think I'm watching footage of actual events just because the quality sucks. The camera and the way it's used is easily comparable to the attributes a TV camera of this day and age. With Rec, if I wasn't trying to enjoy a movie(as I should), I can easily feel as if I'm watching a play.As far as script and plot, of course it was the same for the most part, but this was in no way a shot-for-shot remake as some claim. They changed many scenes and made them a lot more believable. The camera man, for one, gets off his lazy butt a lot more instead of just watching people get mangled. And there are a lot more things, some even very subtle, that feel a lot more logical than in the original. The movie as a whole makes a lot of since, which a lot of horror tends to loose grip of. These were believably scared people doing things that scared people do with no over the top 'trip and fall' routines.The bottom line is I can't deny that Quarantine was scary even after just watching its inspiration and the sequel to that (I don't usually get scared). People need to just stop crying about how they don't want originals to be touched and look at the bright side; They didn't mess it up. If the makers of the original didn't want them to make this movie, they wouldn't have let them.If Quarantine was made first, this argument would switch right around; and Rec would look like a silly imitation. Both Rec and Quarantine are undoubtedly two of the best horror films I have ever seen; and I don't like many. Which was better? If it came down to it, I'd say 'Rec' only because I'm always on the original's side. And also, I like the Spanish lead in Rec more; she's really hot.</fullReview>

For those not up to deciphering the exact plot, just look up the definition of the title of the movie, and you'll have a real good idea. A journalist who is shadowing a pair of firemen accompanies them on a call to a building where the residents have heard strange noises coming from the apartment of an elderly resident.Within moments of their arrival, a police man on scene has his throat bitten, a fireman is bitten and falls off the stairway, and all hell breaks loose when they can't evacuate the victims of this elderly maniac because the CDC and the national guard have arrived on the scene to quarantine the building. On a side note, isn't it a tad ironic that at a time when the American nightmare is losing your home, people are being forced to remain inside? Elements of claustrophobia are explored, along with sexism early on in the film, but ultimately what the film is about is an apparent strain of rabies with an onset so rapid that the infected are like zombies who are on the prowl to kill the uninfected.Somehow, even though they are not residents of the building and are thus new to the environment, the two survivors at the end are the journalist and the camera man. Among the peculiar discoveries they make is an apartment that has been converted into a make shift lab, complete with animal testing, beakers and flasks of peculiar chemicals, and newspaper clippings about doomsday cults Yeah, way to throw in some curve ball there guys! On a You Tube review, another viewer suggested that its never a good sign for a movie to show the concluding scene in the commercials advertising it, and you know, he's right. In fact, although Jennifer Carpenter is more than serviceable as a modern day scream queen, this generation of horror film maker seems to have been raised on the slasher and zombie horror of the 80's. The only problem is, it isn't the 80's. Its not even the 90's! If you go to see this film, what you can expect is LOTS of shaky camera shots that do not really allow you to focus on any one thing, a lot of running, a lot of screaming, and a fair bit of gore. Again though, for you gore fiends, the shaky camera does limit your ability to fully appreciate just how gory it would appear that some of the scenes are! In short, this film has been made to capitalize on the success of films like "The Blair Witch Project" and "Cloverfield," however its neither as witty or as campy as those films are, and the storyline wears very, very thin very fast.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>An Enhanced Version of the Original</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>"Angela Vidal" (Jennifer Carpenter) is a young television reporter who is collecting material for a night-time show in Los Angeles. In this particular episode she and her cameraman, "Scott Percival" (Steve Harris) are sent to a fire station to film an ordinary night with the fire department. When a call is received they decide to follow two firemen who are assigned to the case. Upon arriving at an apartment complex they notice that two policemen have also just made it to the scene and so they follow them inside. Once inside they all go upstairs to find an old woman covered in blood and screaming. She then bites one of the policemen in the throat and after pulling her off they evacuate downstairs taking the injured policeman with them. Not long afterward one of the firemen is also attacked and thrown down the staircase suffering serious injuries as well. At this point the decision is made to evacuate the apartment complex but they find that they have been locked inside and are trapped with the residents. Now, while it is tempting to say that this film is a "carbon copy" of the movie "Rec", the fact is it would be more appropriate to call this movie an "enhanced version" of the original. Also, while I personally didn't see a need for a remake, this particular picture has certain attributes that are worth mentioning. First, "Quarantine" is a more polished version than "Rec". The film is of a higher quality and the camera work is less shaky. And while "Rec" had a more realistic feeling, the attention to detail in "Quarantine" made it more viewable in my opinion. Additionally, "Rec" was filmed in Spanish and dubbed in English which is, in all honesty, never quite as good as having everything in a language a person understands. So, on those scores I suppose "Quarantine" rates slightly higher. On the other hand, those viewers who have already seen "Rec" probably won't get the same thrill out of "Quarantine" because they already know what transpires before it even happens. Be that as it may, both films are quite good and I believe that most zombie enthusiasts will probably enjoy either one. I know I did.</fullReview>

		<username>Polaris_DiB</username>
		<score>0.9</score>
		<shortDescription>Best executed first person shooter I have seen yet--but I haven't seen the original</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>I wanted to hold off watching this until I saw the original Spanish version, REC, but it was on television the other day and for lack of reading material and an Internet connection I decided to sit it out. It's really a shame that I saw it on television, too, because the commercial breaks really cut into the fantastic execution of a slowly building horror movie. This movie deserves some kudos for being able to consistently build rising tension from the carefree and playful beginning to the tiny tension-filled space of its conclusion. It may not be the most original story, but it is thus far the best executed first person shooter currently out there.I like the first person shooter horror genre. Ever since Blair Witch Project came out, I've been game, even though it is very much so a genre with massive hits and massive misses. For what it's worth so far, Diary of the Dead gets my vote for most meaningful and intelligent, but it really falls apart in execution, best shown by the introductory, "I have decided to put music over it to make it more engaging." Uh, what? Quarantine avoids music entirely (awesome) and, best of all, is essentially 28 Days Later in a very confined space.A VERY confined space.This, to me, equates quality survival horror. Not only are the characters trying to escape rabid reanimated corpses, but they are also stuck with each other, forcing the human element out as panic takes over and characters get into fist-fights over simple arguments. The heart is aflutter with glee just thinking about it. Here, it's even more fun because while you want the characters to survive, you also have the outsider perspective of the quarantine itself, meaning you also want the characters to be contained. Outbreak, claustrophobia, mob rule, the state of emergency, and fast zombies all in what looks and feels like less than a thousand foot square space, accelerated at a brilliantly constant pace.Which means I am even more incentives to see the Spanish original, though with understanding that there's going to be some differences in approach, and thus realizing that this might, just might, be a case where the remake ousts the original. I say this because one of the absolute best parts of Quarantine is the cameraman as character who at one point even uses the camera as a weapon to smash a zombie head to pulp. Think the hilariousness and pure visceral pleasure of the Yellow Man's untimely end in Sin City from the perspective of Bruce Willis' fist. YES. It is that awesome. If the original doesn't have that, then better or worse Quarantine still gets my vote for the "Why hadn't anybody thought of that yet?" moment right there. According to my compatriot hanging out nearby, he hadn't heard me laugh so gleefully since Brad Pitt got it to the face in Burn after Reading.This movie deserves a little more appreciation than just that, however. I have already mentioned the tension building but I have neglected to mention the shot composition. One of the main reasons why I find Diary of the Dead to be a success over the likes of Blair Witch Project and Cloverfield is that George Romero is smart enough to let the so-called student filmmakers actually know how to hold the camera steady long enough to actually show something. In Quarantine, the cameraman character is a professional and for the majority of the movie keeps things pretty composed, even if it's unrealistic to imagine he'd be capable of doing it while running away in a panic from vicious violent monsters. Take it from a professional video technician himself, it's really hard to run along and remember to zoom in and out while you're doing it, much less the snapping zooms featured here (you can, even accidentally, use the servo zoom for most prosumer cameras, but to get snap zooms like the one's featured here usually requires constant operation of the manual zoom with a second hand). However, at least throughout most of the movie you can actually see what's going on, and the frames get more broken and jittery in perfect correlation to the tension. By the end, the light on the end of the camera is flickering on and off, the cameraman is shaking, the zoom is wild, and the effect is like what annoys most people about this genre, watching a strobe-light instead of a film, but again I do not believe this movie overuses it in hiding other elements of the movie like production design and, best of all, the mad scientist reveal at the end.Anyway, I'm pleasantly surprised how enjoyable this movie was and now I'm quite anxious to see the original. It is a shame that this movie contains the stigma of a remake but if the original is going to wow me, it will probably be because the thin plot of the remake has a stronger development or drive in the original to achieve the same ends. I suppose I will see, in the meantime, I can't help but recommend this one as a success taken on its own.--PolarisDiB</fullReview>
	</review>

		<fullReview>"Quarantine" is a horror film that follows the concept of one single camera capturing the details of a strange and frightening happening. "Quarantine" is the remake of the French film named "Rec." (which unfortunately I haven't seen it) and that leads to many comparison among viewers. I'll won't do this here because of that but I will compare it with some of the other movies I mentioned and it's place in horror films, what it represents. One reporter (Jennifer Carpenter) and her cameraman (Steve Harris) are making a special report with some L.A. firefighters when suddenly they're called for an emergency call. When they get to the place of the emergency (a building) they realize that something strange is happening after a old lady attacks a cop by biting him and after that zombie creatures appears sucking blood from the residents of the building. At last they're quarantined without knowing exactly what's going on. From beginning until end the movie is showed by the point of view of the cameraman. So be prepared for a hand held and shaky (and not annoying at all) camera trembling, filming with almost no kind of illumination (specially in the last minutes). And with that an interesting mystery about what happens to the people on the building, the quarantine scheme and the aftermath with the creatures running around and killing everyone. I know that it was a tense situations and most of the characters are angry, not calm and all but she over reacts in some moments. First there's a call, firefighters go see what it is, police already was there, the old lady attacks and all of sudden...quarantine! In the middle of this situation (when some CDC guys enter in the building) it is said that the people from CDC (Center for Disease Control) knew something was happening in the building after tracking a sick dog who lived in there with a family. So how could they possibly know that the disease already had spread? They simply just put the building in quarantine and allowed one of their representantives to go in there. That guy never returned. They send more? No. Why? Who knows? But they ordered the Army to shoot people who were trying to get out of the building. Other thing that really bothered me was the disabled old man mentioned in one conversation but he never appeared in the film (a plot hole). When everything started and the police started to count the people in the building someone says that the African couple has a old man in their apartment, a paralytic man and the policeman wants him to be brought where everybody is. But people keep saying no, he might have spread the disease. Just because is he on a wheelchair. What is it? Are we in 1919 when just saying poliomyelitis was scary enough to people stay away? That was totally pointless and prejudiced.Despite these minor flaws I thought it was a great film, very well made and very entertaining. The cast worked good (specially Jay Hernandez as one of the firefighters) but for some audiences it might be a distractive thing because of some known names (but I'm not counting Johnathon Schaech because he's unrecognizable here). Until there I'm still gonna think this movie deserves a view, it's quite good. Nothing similar as the other movies I mentioned though. Director John Dowdle had done his homework, and most likely realized why the foe-mentioned reality horror films didn't quite work on a massive level, and corrected the problems. Quarantine has some down right creepy moments, and keeps the viewer on edge through out most of the film. You can certainly feel the dread of the characters and place yourself into the situation, which is why it worked for me. Once the horror starts, it seldom lets up, and that is what makes Quarantine a much better film then it's predecessors. Highly dramatic segments that make you feel like your in a spook-house, just waiting for something to jump out at you...and they do! Solid performances and writing, my only gripe would have to be with the constant shaky-cam which always pisses me off, but I can at least understand why it's there, because the characters are running for their lives through out 90% of the film.. Well done and certainly worth a gander by any true horror fan.</fullReview>

		<username>Jack` Harding</username>
		<score>0.5</score>
		<shortDescription>A hollow horror victory, but a victory nonetheless. I guess?</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>People who moan about the 'current' Hollywood thirst for remakes tend to forget that there's nothing 'current' about it: Hollywood's been remaking movies for decades. Especially horrors. Be that as it may, the enormity of this age-old vogue is well on the brink of futility. I mean, do films like Halloween, The Omen, Amityville, A Nightmare on Elm Street, Psycho and Friday the 13th really need to be remade? Course not. Do "foreign" films like The Grudge, The Eye, The Ring, Pulse, Premonition, Dark Water and One Missed Call? Course not. Yet it comes as no surprise that the scariest ever by product of Spanish cinema,(rec), was seized and canned by Hollywood before it even hit the screens back last April.With the exception of a new title, cast, language and location, not much else has changed. Instead of Madrid, 2008, it's Los Angeles, 2008, and a small team of TV reporters are following a squad of firemen on a routine night shift. The footage is completely live and the first job of the night is to rescue a helpless old lady who is trapped inside her apartment: a standard call, a simple call. Or is it? This, after all, is a brutal concoction of terror and realism- a horror film. Hence, it all goes Pete Tong: the old dear loses the plot and a zombie virus takes over the building.Now, the only thing that matters is getting the hell out of this high-rise hell house but wait, it's been cordoned off by the LAPD: quarantined. What are the odds!? The only thing left for the uninfected to do is scrounge the place for an exit; to run, hide and try to survive. To make a break for the roof, to kill as many zombies as they can and keep that camera rolling.Quarantine, then, is a near replica of Jaume Balanguero and Paco Plaza's much better hand-held opus (rec). A film so simple yet shrewd, so edgy and erratic that the viewer is never unaware of the inevitable sledgehammer blows that shake and shock throughout the fleeting yet ruthless plot. Here, director John Erick Dowdle has done an OK job. His Quarantine echoes all but everything that made (rec) tick including its unforgettable last act (a horrific stairway chase followed by a near unwatchable, night vision take of a deserted penthouse containing nothing but the answers behind the film's mystery and a gangly, malnourished zombie who comes out of the dark and scares you silly. Films like The Blair Witch Project, Cloverfield, (rec) and, now, Quarantine are what I liked to call 'cinematic marmite'- they evoke extreme reactions. Some love, some hate. The reasons to love are reasons to hate- raw, course and tangible film-making that blends truth with trickery, encouraging the viewer to empathise and participate whether they want to or not. Yes, the shaky 'discovered footage' gambit has become a bit of a gimmick but when it works- it works. Quarantine will enthral just as many as it'll appal.Final Verdict: Given the fact that (rec) relied little on the conviction and frequency of on screen dialogue, a virtual frame-for-frame, act-for-act remake was unnecessary in cinematic terms and nothing a little dubbing (or jazzy Slumdog styled subtitles) couldn't have handled. Like (rec) and Cloverfield, Quarantine shuns the Blair Witch's slow boiling paranoia approach and goes straight for the jugular. The outcome is quite horrific yet a clear photocopy but it's done so with such clout and pace, you won't care.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<shortDescription>If you haven't seen the original, this is a fun ride.</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>REC was an insane movie, a nonstop, free roaming white knuckle thrill ride that doesn't let up for its entire running time. Quarantine, while not in the same league, manages to retain that feel and that pacing that worked so well in the original. For anybody that doesn't know REC was a small independent Spanish film that opened in very few cinemas here in the UK. For those that did get a chance to see it in the cinema it was a swift, terrifying roller-coaster with one of the stomach curling, horrifying ending of all time. I couldn't give the movie enough praise, it managed to take an old formula and reinvent itself.Quarantine is the American remake, shot for shot and unchanged in so many ways it's nearly identical to its Spanish originator. What allowed me to like this movie so much more than I wanted to, was because while most American remakes cannot do justice to their sources. Quarantine caters for that audience that missed out on it first time round.It's because this movie is shot for shot that fans of the original will not be offended with what this movie does but it also makes it obvious that this is not a movie catered for that audience.Hopefully this will have the effect where those that enjoy what this movie brings to the table and walks out of the cinema having not seen REC shaken and stirred. Hopefully they will be enticed to seek RECout on DVD. Quarantine as a movie, isn't perfect. One of the reasons the Spanish is a better film is that this film, it feels put on. It feels as if these actors are merely going through the motions whilst with the original you were more engrossed. It really allowed you to feel for these characters and it was upsetting when their time came.Also this version of the film it undoubtedly less authentic, unlike REC which when to the extremes of casting a real TV Presented to play set role, here everything is set up and while it manages to retain its frights to a outstanding degree it never surpasses the realism the first one conveyed. To the films advantage, it's a bloodthirsty movie, blood and guts seep from every crevice the environments are claustrophobic and so very dank, despite all of the flaws Quarantine was destined to face upon release. It's certainly not a bad film. It retains its dark, creepy atmosphere and while not to the same effect as REC it manages to scare even those who have already seen the original. Jenifer Carpenter does a admirable job a bringing out the raw humanity of her character but once again not to the same effect Manuela Velasco did this time last year.Verdict: It's an admirable effect, a one that will ultimately go unrecognized. If you've already seen REC you don't need to see this movie. If you haven't it should go to the top of your list of movies to see in the cinema because that is the best place to watch it. A frenzied full blooded adrenalin ride pumped with steroids, far from perfect, but terrifying none the less.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>Quaratine all remakes</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Quarantine is a remake of a 2007 Spanish film called REC.Usual story of humans zombies infecting one another with the "disease" and standard chaos ensues. The only difference here is that instead of a world wide pandemic the action takes place inside a small block of apartments.This is handy as it builds up the claustrophobic tension.PLOT: TV crew follow a group of firemen for the night filming their activities and it all goes swimmingly until they are called to a disturbance in the most run down block of apartments in history which are inhabited by the usual horror movie character clichés Once all the clichés are rounded up in the main entrance we learn that there is a nuclear threat from some guys in space suits and they all need to give a blood sample (WTF nuclear blood!!) to prove that they are clean.A group of arrogant Americans are unable to stand still for long enough to allow this simple procedure to take place and amongst the chaos that they mostly created for themselves the one infected old woman manages to start the contagious "pass the infection on" game.The Spanish version managed to do this with a smaller cast, noticeably smaller budget, little blood splattering violence that American Horror films have come to know and over use and with this they created a film that was scary as hell, with a real sense of panic amongst the entire cast.The American version screwed up the two most successful scares in the Spanish version in an almost scene for scene remake.The Americans had a larger cast but only to serve the purpose of killing them off in the most violent ways possible. The key fact that the American version missed out on is violence does not make for a scary film. It is the build-up of tension and suspense which make the scares more enhanced when they do come :O Overall I would give: REC 9/10 as it is one hell of a film Quarantine 6/10 as it was a pointless remake of a Spanish film for the sole reason that it would make more money if it were in English to an English Speaking audience. 		<shortDescription>"Quarantine pushes the senses... making Blairwitch look like Gilligan's Island..." - Tom Millan "Kindred Gem Films 1998"</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Great acting in this psychological and literal "horror fest". Jennifer Carpenter's performance was riveting with a final score of excellent... All of the other actors, no exceptions, were well versed and played their roles with the utmost sincerity and intrigue- they were more than believable. Great directing as well in this feature as they paid extreme attention to detail, leaving no stone unturned when it came to all of the technical audio and visual aspects of this film.. Jennifer Carpenter, infamously known as Deborah Morgan on "Dexter", does a 180 degree turn in this film to play an innocent and excited TV reporter caught in a horrific situation. Her and her camera man tag along with firefighters who end up at an apartment which soon ends up becoming infested with a rash of rabies driven psychos. The film is extremely visceral and dark. As well done as this movie was I thought some of the madness and dark elements of the film went over the top. It was overdone. What is horrific of the film is not only the literal elements, but the psychological features it provides. The viewer is powerless to stop the mayhem that unravels around the characters and may even desire, more than once, to jump into the screen to rescue the characters- bringing them back into the safe haven of the living room. But that doesn't happen. With the extreme anger and rage between people, plus confusion, fear and panic- the viewer is helpless as bodies begin to drop by viral infected savages. These are some of the elements that can cause visceral stress to some viewers and should seriously not be viewed by the faint hearted. Some viewers may even get sick.Another psychological element of the film which can be stressing and perhaps troublesome to some is the threat of a real outbreak of biological terror by some strange religious cult. This is quite prevalent with examples of David Koresh and the Branch Davidians, or the Manson Family of the 1960's, or the religious cult in Japan who tried to release a doomsday virus in the subway system some years back. The makeup and stunts are extremely believable and the extreme violence and emotion is not to be underestimated in this film. This film does not mess around. The beginning is slow paced, giving the viewer the chance to know all of the characters and grow somewhat attached to some, depending on the viewer. Make no mistake: once the film picks up speed be prepared to go on a visceral, gory, disturbing and extremely violent roller coaster ride- which won't stop until the very end.. The viewer becomes helpless to stop the onslaught of increasing tension, hostility and violence which, to some viewers, may only increase their depression levels. The viewer is powerless to stop it.This is why the film is so well done. It is not for everyone. "Quarantine" tests the senses and pushes the viewers emotions over the edge. That's raw and real the acting and directing is. I docked a point because as I said before some of the elements are overdone. This is the third film I have seen which uses the "Blairwitch" style of film making which entails a "one eye" view from the camera. "Cloverfield" was another film which used this style- another film which was well executed in my opinion. Giving the movie a bad rating because it freaked you out is not a proper rating. Rate the film on it's artistic merits. Rate the film when it comes to the writing, directing, acting and technical avenues such as sound, lighting, blocking etc.I was impressed with this film. Four stars of five.Tom Millan "independent film director/ actor" Kindred Gem Films 1998</fullReview>

		<username>Neil Welch</username>
		<score>0.7</score>
		<shortDescription>Decent horror</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Quarantine is based on a simple concept - a TV crew (comprising perky girl frontman and cameraman) shadowing a firecrew for a fly-on-the wall TV show follow them to a building where screams have been heard. No sooner do they enter the building than the authorities arrive and prevent anyone leaving. It turns out that there is an extremely virulent rabies variant in the building which turns people into savage mindless flesh-ripping monsters pretty quickly after they've been bitten themselves. So the question is which order will the people locked in go down in, and will our pretty girl reporter survive? This film's main strength - the simplicity and power of the basic concept - also leads into its main weakness. Some care is taken at the start to present the main characters to us at the fire station - we meet them, get to know a little about them, and like them - but then, once the mayhem starts, we have what one might call the Cloverfield problem: hand-held camera and low light mean that it is difficult to follow what is happening, and to who. This undoubtedly adds to the cine-verite feel of the film but there are times when it becomes frustrating.The largely unknown cast does well (the only one I recognised was Greg Germann, Richard Fish from Allie McBeal), although Jennifer Carpenter becomes a little wearing in the closing ten minutes or so - not her fault, and very well performed, but I'd had enough by the end.Talking of the last ten or fifteen minutes, I thought this was the weakest section. There is some business with a reel to reel tape recording which gave the impression that some explanation would be forthcoming, but nothing was needed, so why even go there? And how come the machine worked when the power was off? And why didn't the camera operator recognise that the sound might mean that the tape recorder should be switched to a faster speed? I was also a little bemused by the fact that the quasi-rabies virus appeared to cause the victims to sprout zombie teeth (insofar as you were ever able to get a clear look at them). And there was a shot in the trailer which should NOT have been there as it caused a major spoiler for the ending.I must also say that there was some laughing in the cinema at some of the more violent, brutal parts, although this might be a comment on the audience rather than the movie itself, which managed to be atmospheric, tense, and realistic from the moment the incidents in the apartment building started.This was a decent horror - it delivered on its promise.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<username>ametaphysicalshark</username>
		<score>-1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>Entertaining, but rarely chilling</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>"Quarantine" is a remake of the Spanish film "Rec", which has gained a large cult following and even plenty of critical appreciation. I unfortunately was busy during "Rec"'s only screening in my city so I went into "Quarantine" without being able to compare it to the original film. I imagine the original film is fairly similar, given that the film is shot from the perspective of a news crew, but the acting and writing may very well be quite different, and perhaps better.I suppose it would be incorrect to call "Quarantine" a zombie movie, as the 'zombies' are still technically alive, but considering what the 'zombies' do in the movie they are still basically zombies. They bite, they groan, and do other zombie-like things. The movie opens with a news crew shooting a special on the local fire department. Late that night the department gets a call, they respond, the news crew and the firemen enter the building where there is some rabies-like disease spreading and end up trapped in there, 'quarantined' if you will. The first few scenes at the fire department have already been compared to the similarly uneventful opening scenes in "Cloverfield", but Drew Goddard's script for that film was far, far superior to this one, which is packed with stilted, unrealistic dialogue. Of course, people complained about the characters in "Cloverfield" being annoying, because they could have been real people, they talked like real people, and were annoying like most real people are, so I imagine those people will be pleased that we get CHARACTERS here. Yep, CHARACTERS defined by the character outlines. Simply not very good writing.All that said, the first stages of the film are actually quite engaging and suspenseful, and the development of the events at the quarantined building is very intriguing, and the first few kills are pretty surprising and fun. Of course, the camera does shake quite often in "Quarantine", but other than the last half hour which sees the movie degenerate from suspenseful horror thriller to what a faithful "House of the Dead" movie might have looked like (it literally largely consists of zombies randomly appearing in front of the camera, and at one point I instinctively pressed on my coke bottle; I must have been subconsciously reminded of the experience of playing that game at arcades), with all the motion you can expect from that sort of perspective, the camera is relatively un-shaky compared to "Blair Witch" or "Cloverfield". The most relevant comparison is to "Diary of the Dead" (both zombie movies shot this way), and there's really no question that "Quarantine" is superior in almost every way imaginable to Romero's latest (disappointing) effort."Quarantine" gets off to a reasonable start, picks up some real momentum, then turns into a live action video game, and as everyone knows it's no fun watching others playing a video game, and the lead actress' histrionics get quite aggravating. The movie on the whole is short enough to be harmless and entertaining, but given the quality of the concept and the middle section of the film, I'm disappointed that the director and writers missed the chance to make this a genuinely chilling and claustrophobic film, and it really ends up being more an action film than a horror film.Oh, and there's an absolutely shameful ripoff of the greatest 'jump' moment of all time from Robert Wise's 1963 film "The Haunting".6/10</fullReview>
	</review>

		<shortDescription>What is with all the hate for this movie?</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Guys, just because it's an American remake doesn't automatically mean it will be crap.It's NOT a shot for shot remake, either. The tweaks, little changes, added scares, and altered reason for why it's happening in the first place addressed the original's weak spots and fixed them to great effect. 		<username>sddavis63 (revsdd@gmail.com)</username>
		<score>0.9</score>
		<shortDescription>Really Edge Of Your Seat Stuff</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>By the time this movie is finished you're almost exhausted! This is truly a movie that, aside from the first few scenes, really does keep you on the edge of your seat waiting for the next bit of horror to envelop both the characters and you, the viewer. Those opening first few scenes are really quite effective in and of themselves as well. They establish the basic structure of the movie. Angela (wonderfully played by Jennifer Carpenter) is a TV reporter who's doing a ride-along with a couple of LA fire fighters one night. Those opening first scenes are shot in the firehouse, and consist mostly of light banter between the crew and the firemen, and actually some interesting footage of an LA fire station, while Angela hopes for a call that will give some good footage for her station. When the call comes, she (and the fire fighters) are totally unprepared for it.The call is to an old building, where an elderly woman is locked in her apartment and won't come out. When the police and firemen finally break down the door they discover the woman clearly sick and out of her mind with what is obviously a rabies-type virus that causes victims to become extremely violent. She ends up having to be shot, and then everyone discovers that the building has been sealed off by the authorities. No one's being allowed out and the virus is starting to spread very quickly - the movie establishing that symptoms start to show up within an hour of exposure. Is there any hope that anyone could survive this?Essentially, this is filmed in the style of "The Blair Witch Project." Angela's cameraman carries his camera everywhere through the mounting terror and eventually gets a video record of it that presumably is going to be found at some point once the authorities get into the building. The movie itself is far more exciting than "The Blair Witch Project" was. I spent significant time on the edge of my seat, because you truly have no idea what's coming next. Under the circumstances, the cameraman is perhaps a bit too conscientious. I don't know that I would have had the self-control to keep filming everything that was happening around me. That might have been a bit of a credibility problem here. The last few scenes are very effective. They offer a hint of an explanation as to what the virus might be (or at least where it might have come from) and the very last scene of the movie reminded me a little bit of the last scene of the movie "Descent" for anyone who's seen that film. It was a different type of scene (the last scene of "Descent" was very gentle and calm, while the last scene here is anything but) but both movies end very powerfully on notes of sheer hopelessness that really do leave you a bit drained. 9/10</fullReview>

There are one to many moments were we wait for the scare but never get it. It sorta feels like a waste of time. In all honesty it isn't that big of a deficit. The violence and gore in the film is frequent but not necessarily inventive. The films best moment of violence actually isn't seen, involves a dog, sprinting into an elevator. You'll have to watch. Id rather no ruin it.All in all Quarantine is a very good film that's pretty fun and quite scary. It certainly has its' unique aspects, and it stands alone as different, unflinching horror film.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>A mixed bag, but the positive does outweigh the negative</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Obviously, this is not Cloverfield. Its form is not as smooth and thought-through, the dialog isn't as memorable, and information isn't revealed in the same manner... in short, the J.J.(well, when the man is at his best, anyway) that was evident in aforementioned, is not here. That's fine, not everything has to be done by him(there certainly are people who'd petition against that). Also, they realize this, and do the smart thing: They don't try to copy him, but rather go in a different direction, albeit using the same basic tool. The POV-film is incredible, specifically for horror, if done right. And they sure knew what they were doing, here. How to use the camera, and they particularly worked on the sound, too, which comes off completely natural. The execution was quite good. This doesn't take off immediately, however, once it does, it does not let up. The pacing is fitting. The tone is spot-on and consistent, more or less throughout. The characters are credible and well-written, and their on-screen development does not come off as forced. This can be very effective, the most well-done of the shocks ought to have you jumping in your seat. The least of them tend to not be too bad. This is genuinely suspenseful, instead of just bombarding the audience with gore. The realism is high in this. It has irony, bordering on satire, as well, mainly of the genre and its many clichés. The cinematography and planning are almost invariably great, with a couple of downright golden moments. There is harsh language in all of this, as well as some strong violence(including a few gruesome bits) and/or disturbing content. It was refreshing to see so little(next to no) nudity, much less sexuality, in a new release of this kind. I recommend this to fans of this type of movie, and I'd say that if you go in for an atmospheric, scary flick, and don't expect this to redefine your view of the world, you're likely to largely enjoy the heck out of those 90 minutes(I would advise only watching trailers and, to a lesser extent, looking at the cover if you can forget or at least not think about one particular popularly used image, lest it spoil a surprise). 7/10</fullReview>

		<fullReview>I've seen Blair Witch Project, Cloverfield, and Diary of the Dead. and I'm sure anyone who has watched those movies will almost immediately compare Quarantine to them, like I do.Make no mistake that Quarantine is mostly just a chop-up of other famous horror/suspense titles that are pasted together. The feel of the movie is similar to Blair Witch, the look is similar to Cloverfield, the "zombies" are very similar to what we see in 28 Days Later/28 Weeks later. Although this movie seem to copy/paste a lot, it does it rather well and manages to make a fairly original feel, but just barely.However there is a element to the film that makes it rather original from the other films of similar ilk. Unlike the three aforementioned titles this film is not a long event that takes place over several cuts of the camera. Instead, Quarantine has almost no break in the action (there are breaks were the camera is turned off but these breaks are very brief) This basically means that you are always on the edge of your seat. Always. There's no long dramatic narrations and dialogs either, everything is fast-paced but not too fast that you, the viewer don't pick up on vital information. From start to finish it's a non-stop 100 minute ride. That, by far, is what I think to be the movies biggest merit, mainly because it greatly heightens realism.One other thing I feel I should mention is that the acting in this movie is brilliant. No really, I think it is. I know that horror movies are often panned for lousy acting (which more often that not is justified) but this movie's cast is really good and you'll almost forget that this is a movie.All in all. A fantastic movie if your looking for a good scare. There will be plenty of opportunity for you to be jumping off your seat and screaming in shock.</fullReview>

		<date>2008-10-22</date>
		<username>Jonny_Numb</username>
		<score>0.8</score>
		<shortDescription>Rabies, Motion Sickness, and Raw Terror</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Regardless of its derivative status (it's an American remake of a well-praised Spanish film that has yet to find Stateside distribution), its indulgence of the Found Footage subgenre (recently seen in action in "Cloverfield" and "Diary of the Dead"), and its countless steals from several generations of horror film ("The Silence of the Lambs," "28 Days/Weeks Later," "The Descent," and "The Crazies" are just a few obvious points of reference), nothing can change the fact that "Quarantine" is one of the most frightening films I have ever seen. This is not something I say lightly?as a lifelong fan of the genre, I have sought out the rare, the obscure, and the downright stupid to satiate my inner horror geek?yet few films have sent icily chilled shocks down my spine with such terrifying efficiency. Melding the "you-are-there" POV of hand-held camera, and our post-9/11 fears of biological warfare hitting home, with the current trend of "fast zombie" films, "Quarantine"'s method is the key to its madness (and its impossible-to-suppress effect), and a mere synopsis does the film a great injustice?like other films that push their extremes to surprising levels, this is something to be seen and experienced. And while arbiters of criticism who insist that a motion picture contain some virtue and redemption will condemn "Quarantine" based solely on its nihilism and outcome (the title alone pretty much rules out a Happy Ending), the inevitable unravels with skilled craftsmanship (indeed, it takes a skilled craftsman to make scripted shocks seem off-the-cuff) in a jigsaw-puzzle assembly of "clues" that flash in the background, documents briefly glimpsed on tables, and anachronistic attic rooms that mirror the mad-scientist setups of the Atomic Age without a lick of postmodern irony. The characterizations are cursory, and sensibly so?extensive development would seriously retract the film's primary intention of unsettling the audience with a string of self-contained events that build to a horrifying crescendo (that being said, the non-marquee cast is a game bunch, and lends a refreshingly unglamourous realism that gels well with the proceedings)?throughout "Quarantine," we get the feeling that the events are happening to US as much as the fictional characters on-screen. And unlike other FF shockers like "Cannibal Holocaust," "The Blair Witch Project," and "Diary of the Dead," Dowdle's film (remake or not) succeeds so brilliantly because of its disinterest in the conventions of cinema?with no intention of making the film narratively or aesthetically palatable, "Quarantine" jumps headlong into a visceral abyss that, for 89 unrelenting minutes, shows the viewer no mercy. I'll admit that the final result left me with the beginnings of a motion-sick headache, but I'll be damned if it also didn't leave me more scared and unsettled than I'd ever been in a dark theater.</fullReview>
	</review>

Secondly, Angela, the main character, got really annoying as the movie wore on. She "lost her marbles," whimpering and shaking and crying. Constantly. 		<shortDescription>Simple, basic logic is unusually destructive here, even for a horror movie?</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Quarantine starts off reasonably well, if not at all originally. A small film crew is doing a ride- along with the fire department to get a first hand look at what their daily professional lives are really like. The film is presented the film crew's unedited stock footage, so we get lots of video of the firemen acting not at all like firemen but instead like young men who are living in close quarters for long periods of time. Finally an interesting call comes in and the crew are called out to a building in town. Up to this point, the movie is presented in a style even more realistic than a documentary, because the footage that we see is not even edited. Once they arrive at the building, of course, weird things start happening.You know the premise, the movie is essentially a zombie movie, but a lot of the suspense and tension is predicated on the fact that this group of innocent people have been for some reason barricaded inside a building by the police, government, military, whatever. Soon we find out that it's the Center For Disease Control doing the quarantining, but the point is, the people that are meant to protect us are locking these innocent people inside a building and leaving them for dead and the people inside have no idea why.But here's my first question, how do you lock an entire modern fire crew inside an old, dilapidated building presumably made primarily of plaster and wood? Later they discover that approaching windows is enough to get yourself shot, but after what happened soon after they arrived at the building, I am willing to bet that none of those guys would have been willing to spend more than two or three minutes locked unwillingly inside the building.Next, it seems that breaking through plaster walls is not the only bit of standard fireman training that these guys are lacking. When people start getting hurt, at one point one of the firemen calls out, "Is there a doctor in the house?" I had been under the impression that in order to be a fireman you have to have some emergency medical treatment training, but I could be wrong. I had to do the training in order to be a summer camp counselor a couple years ago, but maybe sleep-away camps are just stricter than your typical major city fire department.Jennifer Carpenter is the lead character, the host of the documentary that the film crew was making. You may remember her from The Exorcism of Emily Rose, the astonishingly scary horror film that she starred in back in 2004. Sadly, Quarantine just doesn't have the story or the content to give her much to do except run around looking scared. Her performance as Emily Rose was one of the best horror performances I've ever seen, but Quarantine just gives the feeling that she's waiting for an opportunity or a good reason to do some real horror acting.Overall the movie is not bad, but it is overwhelmingly casual in its presentation of a typical horror movie, this time with the slight variation that all of the victims are trapped in close quarters with the zombies. They have zombies trying to get them inside the building, and humans on the outside pointing guns at the building to prevent any escape and outbreak of the virus causing the zombiness.There is an interesting scene where the cameraguy actually uses the camera to bludgeon one of the zombies to death, which provides a unique point of view. I had never seen a zombie killed in a movie like that before, but other than that there is just not much special about the movie.</fullReview>

I really expected worse, since I have already seen the great original version, REC on which this remake is based on. But I was pleaseantly surprised that this film didn't suck and was for the most part, almost as good as the original. The remake is an almost exact scene by scene copy of the original, but based in Los Angeles than in Barcelona, Spain where the events of the original REC happened. The only major differences in the remake are that there are more tenants than in the original, the cause of the virus is not supernaturally based as in REC, but a mutant strain of rabies; there is no Medieros girl in the climax in the attic but a very skinny and scary man in the attic played by Doug Jones(Hellboy) and the ethnic composition of the tenants in QUARENTINE is different than the one in REC and there are also about three new scary scenes in the remake that are not in REC such as the"elevator" scene. John Erick Dowdle and his brother,did a very good job adapting the original REC's story into the remake and there are some effectively directed scares in the film. My opinion about this fiim is that QUARENTINE is a good remake but not superior to REC, just like THE RING is a good remake of the original Japanese RINGU but not superior to it as well.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>QUARANTINE (John Erick Dowdle, 2008) **</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Though I had not intended doing so, I ended up watching this right after the cult Spanish horror {.REC} (2007), of which this is a Hollywood remake. While I read comments that it was almost as good as the original, I immediately lost heart, firstly because of a distinctly overbearing, almost immature, protagonist (much more so, in fact, than {.REC}'s Manuela Velasco) played by Jennifer Carpenter (from THE EXORCISM OF EMILY ROSE [2005], though not so that you would notice!).However, there was also the sheer obviousness of the re-imagining - such as making two of its key characters blacks (presumably so as not to elicit race-related criticisms from potential audiences where, in the Spanish film, a narcissistic old man was shamelessly shown passing disparaging remarks about Orientals!). While the film follows the original pretty faithfully, it inserts numerous embellishments which more often than not do not work, thus serve merely to stretch the running-time unnecessarily.Again, some of these are patent Hollywood trademarks: for instance, before the situation goes off the rails, the firemen make bets about whether they can bed Carpenter; the rabid dog only mentioned in {REC.} is still lurking in the condominium; one of the residents refuses to obey orders and suffers the consequences for his actions; perhaps borne out by the bigger budget afforded here, the number of people, amount of nationalities and infected victims concerned is larger (though, like I said, this adds very little of substance).Most importantly, then, the final revelation is not only drastically altered (making it an alien rather than diabolic takeover, involving as it does the so-called "Doomsday Cult"!) but so sketchily presented in this case as to go over the head of most viewers!! In the end, even if the violence is more pronounced in the remake, it is safe to assume that there will be no QUARANTINE 2...</fullReview>
