		<date>2009-04-26</date>
		<username>alanrayford</username>
		<score>0.6</score>
		<shortDescription>Despite an interesting premise, is only middle of the road.</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Ever since seeing "An American Werewolf in London", at the age of five, I have been an aficionado of horror films. For years, the genre constantly delivered the cinematic thrills I instantly became addicted to. But the genre has since fallen on hard times, and things have yet to get better. Put simply, there are simply too many J-Horror film remakes, PG-13 fright flicks, torture porn films, and romanticized vampires for the field to be taken seriously anymore. Sadly, despite having promise, "Quarantine" falls into yet another detrimental category?the gimmick.However, I first want to state that this movie isn't all that bad. The acting, all the way from Jennifer Carpenter to Columbus Short, was pretty good. There was no problem on my part in believing their characters were truly on edge and in dire straits. The set design was also tight. If I lived in California and that building actually existed, I wouldn't mind living there?after it was cleaned up. Also, I have to give props for the lighting and sound. What I could see looked damned good and what I could hear sounded even better. But, and I hate to say it, this film is still mediocre.I felt that the pacing was a bit too uneven. "Quarantine" is approximately ninety minutes long. Normally, this running time would be more than long enough to present a full and satisfying tale. But, seeing as how it spends the first fifteen minutes in a fire station, the pacing is far from smooth. Instead of the situation steadily progressing, it rushes to the worse case scenario in too little time to let things develop. One second the story has two or three infected. Then, in a matter of seconds and off camera, the number of infected persons spirals out of control. Before I had time to adjust to the change in status quo, everyone was running for their lives, and I was left feeling out of the loop. A more gradual shift would've been easier to follow.Also, at several points during "Quarantine", logic just seemed to jump out the window. For one, where were the guns? There were two police officers with sidearms. Later there were two CDC agents with rifles. This counts for four firearms. Yet, only one of the infected was shot. Too me, that made no sense. In one scene, there is an infected person on the other side of a glass door. One character states that the door won't hold. The same character then proceeds to stand right in front of the glass so the infected can reach through and kill him. It was established that this carnage was all due to a contagious pathogen. Then Carpenter's character insists on taking off her jacket and running around in a wife beater? Lastly, a woman is carrying around her sick daughter for what seems like forever. Then, without any warning or change in her condition, the tyke goes evil, attacks her mom, and runs off? But, for me, what really hampers "Quarantine" is it's standing as a gimmick film. From beginning to end, everything is seen from the POV of a hand-held camera. Whatever the camera doesn't see or hear, doesn't really exist?problem. Imagine if "Jaws" had been seen entirely from Chief Brody's POV. We wouldn't have witnessed the opening scene which made the film. We'd never have seen poor Alex Kittner get dragged under the waves in horrific fashion. In "Quarantine", however, this singular focus resulted in me not being able to better understand what the hell was going on. It would've been nice to see more background on this Doomsday Cult. Who were they? Based off the sounds on that tape recorder, what were they? On the flip side, I also never really got to feel anything for the residents of this doomed building, because I wasn't allowed to know who they were or where they were coming from.Dania Ramirez's character gets infected?BFD. A lawyer gets shredded by an infected dog?so what. The lady with the infected daughter is left handcuffed to a staircase where she is attacked?whoop de friggin' do. With them being little more than luggage for the narrative to carry, why should I care about any of them? Normally, in a less claustrophobic movie, this wouldn't matter (case in point "AVP 2"). But, when the environment is so heavily confined and the cast rather small, it sticks out. In other words, it's one thing if I don't care about the majority of a cast. But if I'm not given enough info to care about one character from a cast of twelve, that's something else altogether. And repeated viewings, once the one-time thrill of the gimmick had run its course, didn't answer my questions. If anything, watching this twice only made it more obvious how thin this movie was.Still, "Quarantine" is worth a rental for being a hard R horror flick, with decent production values. I was going to give this a seven for being fun, but having some nagging issues. Sadly, seeing as how it's a remake of a recent foreign film, I have to lower it a point. Hopefully, if they do another horror film, the Dowdle Bros. will abandon the gimmicks of this and the "Poughkeepise Tapes" to focus on crafting an original and more satisfying story. With any luck, maybe it will be the next great sea monster or werewolf flick?because God knows they don't make those anymore.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<shortDescription>Oh no - it's one of them shaky-cam films</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Just like Cloverfield, Diary of the dead, Blair Witch, Cannibal Holocaust and The Planet's Funniest Animals, this is one of those films consisting entirely of footage shot by one of the characters. Thankfully, this one isn't so headache-inducing, in a visual sense at least, although I did find myself grinding my teeth at all the screaming towards the end.What you've got here is a bunch of people sealed in a building where a virus is spreading amongst the tenants. In addition to this you have a couple of policemen, firemen, and an annoyingly insincere reporter with her trusty cameraman by her side. Unable to escape, the cameraman documents the various panic attacks and screaming fits the characters have.It sounds like I'm bashing the film, but I'm not. Despite the 'shakey-cam' angle, and the usual 'characters all screaming at each other' stuff we usually get, Quarantine manages to fit in rather a lot of tense sequences as the tenants try and survive against the odds. About halfway through the film things pick up and the films shifts into high gear right until the end.Perhaps not an incredibly deep or resonant horror film, but you could do a lot worse.Similar title: Mulberry Street - worth tracking down.</fullReview>

		<username>Robert W. (Robert_duder@hotmail.com)</username>
		<score>0.8</score>
		<shortDescription>A much better spin on the hand held "all real" Horror Genre</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>I wanted to see Quarantine from the moment I saw the trailers. I mean I watch almost anything but I love horror and I love films that focus the tension on isolation and Quarantine definitely does that. The story is not really character driven but rather driven by the story and the mystery of what is happening. The decision to film it using the hand held camera technique that other films have used actually worked really well for the film. Previously Cloverfield used this method to decent success and classic Horror like the original Blair Witch film really honed the technique. The key is that it has to really feed the atmosphere and actually make sense to the story and Quarantine has all of that going for it. Unlike Cloverfield the camera is kept relatively steady and clear so it isn't disorientating. Apparently I feel pretty strongly about Asian/European horror films being made into English...but in a positive way. I love The Ring and it's sequel, and The Grudge was solid. Quarantine is part zombie flick, part horror blood bath, part isolated thriller but anyone who likes any of these genre's can't possibly be disappointed with this.Jennifer Carpenter takes the roll of scream queen and does a great job. Sometimes it feels like perhaps she might be over acting but she pulls of terror really disturbingly well. She's a decently strong character and because of the nature of the monster doesn't really get to fight back but she is still a terrific lead character. Steve Harris is her camera man who obviously doesn't get a lot of time in front of the camera that he is in charge of...if any time...but he still plays a decent role with his voice and action and him and Carpenter have decent chemistry which is pretty impressive given they don't show any actual screen time together. The rest of the cast is great, I certainly don't mean to take anything away from anyone but they are all victims plain and straight forward so they get less than five minutes to establish decent characters only to be picked off in horrible ways. There is still some strong actors in these small roles including Jay Hernandez, Johnathon Schaech and Rade Serbedzija (terrific character actor.) No one really stands out unfortunately because this is all about the story.Relative newcomer John Erick Dowdle co-wrote and directed the film. He certainly has a decent eye for horror or thriller and knows how to really get the spirit of the film in motion. The gore is awesome and actually gives you some extremely disturbing images that aren't run of the mill for the horror genre. Considering Quarantine had a very low budget I think they did a truly terrific job at really making it watchable with edge of your seat thrills and a really great story. They could serialize it and might considering the reasonable success of the film but I don't think it would be a good idea as much as I love sequels. One thing is for sure I definitely am looking forward to seeing the Spanish original film .REC Certainly worth checking out!! 8.5/10</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>Fair Remake but Stick with the Original</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Quarantine (2008) ** (out of 4) I'm one who thinks remakes aren't always a bad thing but ever so often you get one that's scene for scene, which is pretty much what this thing here turned out to be. This is a remake of the Spanish film REC from one year earlier and that's certainly the film people should check out. As in the original, a female reporter (Jennifer Carpenter) and her cameraman are hanging out and shooting a documentary with a fire crew when they are called to an apartment. What appears to be your average call turns into a nightmare as everyone gets locked inside as some virus is causing the residents to turn into creatures. This film actually has a lot in common with the remakes of PSYCHO and THE OMEN and that's the rare case where the new version does very little to change anything from the first film. This American remake features the exact same set up, the exact build up and the exact same ending and you really have to ask yourself what the point was. There's a key element to what's going on that's left out here but that doesn't hurt or help the film any more. This thing comes off as a major disappointment considering how great the original film was but it's unfair to judge the two together so I'll just focus on this film. This time out I had a major problem getting involved in the film because not once did I feel any part of the action. Not once did I feel I was trapped inside this apartment complex and this is a major problem. Another problem is that there wasn't an ounce of suspense but this here could be due to me watching this less than 24-hours after viewing the original film. This is a remake where it seems like the filmmakers studied the original for hours and then tried to copy what they watched but in the end the movie feels watered down, weak and just all around tame. There's not a single second in this movie that can compare to the original so I don't see the point of this film. Is it a bad movie? No, it's decent in its own right but there's just not a point to it. I think they could have mixed things up a lot better but in the end it just comes off as rather lazy and uninspired.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>"28 Days Later" meets "The Blair Witch Project" meets "Cloverfield",... with so-so results.</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>QUARANTINE, the remake of Spanish film REC, is one of the latest entries in the horror genre. The plot is quite simple: what begins as a night of footage gathering for a young reporter and her cameraman, going along for the ride with some firemen to a reported emergency at an L.A. apartment building, quickly turns into a hair-raising nightmare. This, because something VERY wrong has happened inside...I cannot say anymore without going into spoiler-hood; however, be warned. This is basically a rehash of THE BLAIR WITCH PROJECT, 28 DAYS LATER and CLOVERFIELD; and the results, while nothing groundbreaking, aren't that bad either -if you can tolerate the extremely shaky hand-held camera without going sick-. The performances are, at the most, serviceable, with Jennifer Carpenter and Jay Hernandez (as a doomed fireman) standing out. Steve Harris is the cameraman, but we mostly hear him rather than seeing him, since he is filming -there are only a couple of really quick shots of him throughout the movie-.I haven't seen the original Spanish film from which they made the remake to establish parameters, but I can say this version delivers the goods satisfactorily; and particularly, the last 10 minutes of the film will have you literally at the edge of your seat.Give yourself a chance and watch it, if you tolerate a good little horror flick with some shaky shots. 		<date>2008-10-18</date>
		<username>dee.reid</username>
		<score>0.8</score>
		<shortDescription>Scary...</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>In the 21st-century generation of horror where kids today are used to the likes of "Saw" and "Hostel," it seems nice to finally see a film like "Quarantine," a horror movie that for the most part eschews mindless blood and gore and instead focuses on scaring the living daylights out of you. (I am, however, guilty of having a weakness for the "Saw" films, and I do intend on seeing "Saw V" next week.) In today's times, that's a rare feat, and "Quarantine" succeeds despite a few missteps along the way. And for this upcoming Halloween season, I felt I needed a good scare, and, boy, I got it.I've never seen the original Spanish film "Rec" from which "Quarantine" is based, but I can say that the filmmakers - chiefly director John Erick Dowdle and his brother/co-screenwriter Drew - have crafted a creepy and effectively terrifying horror/thriller that is able to remind genre fans that Hollywood still has a little bit of what it takes to genuinely frighten us.Like its Spanish source material "Rec" and using the reality-style documentary techniques of "The Blair Witch Project" (1999) and "Cloverfield" (2008) that have become so popular lately, "Quarantine" follows Los Angeles news reporter Angela Vidal (a surprising Jennifer Carpenter) and her cameraman Scott (Steve Harris) as they shadow a fire engine truck on its nightly rounds across the city. Things are pretty slow and uninteresting, but we get to know Angela and Scott and their primary subjects, firemen Jake (Jay Hernandez) and Fletcher (Johnathan Schaech).While on their nightly rounds, Jake's fire truck receives the first call to respond to an incident involving an old woman locked in her apartment in a downtown apartment complex. The police are also on the scene to aid the firemen and get to the bottom of what's going on. Many of the residents are huddled into the building's main lobby. On the third floor, they locate the apartment in question, and its sole inhabitant, and they break down the door, not knowing that doing so would be like unlocking a Pandora's Box of unrelenting terror. What's even more alarming is, why has the CDC (Centers for Disease Control and Prevention) showed up to seal off the building, and why are the police and military threatening to kill anyone who attempts to escape? So Angela, Scott, Jake, and the residents of the building have to try to survive the night in an inescapable building with an unspeakable terror...This is a pretty effective horror/thriller we have here. I'm not lying. "Quarantine" is the first horror film since "The Descent" (2005) - one of the very best horror/thrillers of the new millennium so far, and one of my personal favorite horror films of all time - to truly frighten me in ways I didn't think were any longer possible. But "Quarantine" is by no means perfect. There are a number of times when characters have opportunities to try to survive longer, but ignore them, and I found myself saying in my mind, "You better take a better look around!" or something along those lines. And then of course, as with all reality-style documentary films of late, problems can be found with the first-person camera-work. For those of you who suffer from motion sickness (I don't), the frantic and disorientating camera-work can be a bit of a distraction and kill the mood and your overall enjoyment of the picture.However, there are many other pluses. First is Jennifer Carpenter. It's amazing the transformation she goes through, from first being a clear-headed and bright newswoman to a scream-queen who eventually succumbs to the unrelenting terror of the situation. It's a performance I found to be truly skillful, especially in the later scenes, even though I do question her judgment in some of the later horror sequences. But what I did like is the fact that even when she's screaming her head off, she remains a three-dimensional character, and that's an aspect of her performance that I found to be truly admirable, especially for this sort of movie. (Is she the future of Hollywood/"B"-movie scream-queens?) The biggest surprise, though, has to go to the direction of John Erick Dowdle. Despite any grievances, he is able to structure "Quarantine" with a mounting sense of fear and dread from the get-go, and skillfully maintains it throughout the entire picture. It's these same unbearable levels of fear and dread - and later, pure pitch-black darkness (in one particularly frightening sequence that reminded me of a similar scene from "Aliens") - that keep you on the edge on your seat, and knowing that nobody is going to make it out of this movie alive. And it is for this reason that I gave "Quarantine" the rating that I gave it.That, my friends, is what horror is - sometimes - really about.8/10</fullReview>
	</review>

		<username>Jamie Ward</username>
		<score>0.7</score>
		<shortDescription>Compelling insignificance.</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>For the sake of avoiding repeating myself I'm simply going to discuss the changes and new elements of Quarantine that differ from those of the original. Seeing as the movie is essentially a direct copy of REC I advise -if you want a review of the basic structure and form of the film- that you read my original article.Anyone who has seen Gus Van Sant's now infamous remake of classic movie Psycho should be able to grasp the basic premise of this review. Quarantine, which is essentially a carbon copy remake of this year's suspense laden REC, just about pulls out most of the tricks that the filmmakers of the original did half a year ago, but at the expense of any compelling degree of originality. Of course, to anyone who hasn't seen Hitchcock's Psycho, Van Sant's would be a nice treat; that is, essentially, the aim of what those remaking movies try to do. While covering an already established feature shot by shot for the sake of bringing it to a modern audience doesn't necessarily make for a negative experience, it certainly makes for a less interesting and significant one than seeing it the first time round. This is where the job of reviewing such movies becomes perplexing; Quarantine in most regards is every bit as strong as the source material that it bases itself upon, but the changes that are made only detract further, and when used with scenes that are lifted directly from something I seen only six months ago, the result is naturally unsatisfying.Following the same basic premise as its source, Quarantine tells the story of an amateur news reporter and her camera man as they are locked inside a building where people are going crazy and eating other residents. The story is for the most part identical, save for a few minor details and shift in focus. Of course this is a good thing; REC had an interesting and intriguing premise that always kept the viewer guessing whilst on the edge of their seat. Quarantine is more or less the same. The biggest and most notable exception to this however lies in the pacing of John Erick Dowdle's version, which, at an excessive twenty minutes longer than REC, moves at a much slower rate. One of the original movie's biggest flaws was that while it was only seventy minutes long, there wasn't enough plotting to sustain interest all the way through; particularly in the middle act. Adding twenty minutes onto this version only makes matters worse; the experience is less frantic as a result and as such neglects much of the film's later construction of past paced momentum to that of middling tedium.Characterisation is also notably out of focus; the basic exposition material of the first act remains the same and characters are still given brief snapshots of profiling here and there, but the focus and pacing on these elements is lacking. Of course the original feature didn't have many character moments either, but they were enough to deliver the movie's main selling point of pure horror; Quarantine is less concerned with atmosphere and more about things jumping out into the camera. There are some nice results of this shift which helps make the movie feel distant from its brother, but more often than not, this change in dynamic only hurts the tense atmosphere that made for a terrifying experience six months back. The performances are much more consistent and work every bit as well ?if not better- than those found in the original with Jennifer Carpenter filling in her screaming damsel character with the required conviction to carry the movie forwards.So how can I recommend a copy of another movie when I could simply advise you to watch the first version, which although only marginally superior, nevertheless gets the job done with more ferocity and imagination? The answer is that I cannot, no matter how good Quarantine is. Instead, unless you take allergic reactions to subtitles, I can only recommend that you save your money for a DVD version of REC instead; the differences are subtle but for all intents and purposes make the remake disposable in comparison. If however you have already seen the original then there is absolutely no reason for you to catch this. A carbon copy with only minute changes, Quarintine is a fine example of horror and does its job amply in comparison with its source material, but refusing to do much outside of changing the performers and their language, the movie boils down to one of compelling insignificance.- A review by Jamie Robert Ward (http://www.invocus.net)</fullReview>
	</review>

		<username>happyendingrocks</username>
		<score>0.8</score>
		<shortDescription>Thrilling, scary, and gruesome... no matter what language it's in</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>I've stumbled across a lot of backlash against this film, and most of it is centered around the notion that Quarantine is a "rip-off" of a Spanish film called REC. One IMDb user admitted that they only watched part of Quarantine, but they still branded it with a one-star rating, citing the idea I've just stated as sufficient proof that this film is somehow worthless.So, is Quarantine a rip-off of REC? I can't tell you that, because I haven't seen REC. What I can tell you is that it is now common practice for American studios to purchase the rights to foreign films and make English language versions of them for Western audiences. Surely, even the most casual film-goer should be well aware of this, and horror fans in particular should be intimately familiar with the trend if they've seen any genre offerings made in the last decade. The Ring: remake. The Grudge: remake. Cloverfield: remake. And so on and so on.Arguing that Quarantine shouldn't have been made, because all someone has to do to see it is watch REC and spend the entire film reading subtitles, is ignoring the fact that American mass audiences don't enjoy trying to read and watch a movie at the same time. We could spend all day debating what this says about American film-goers, especially considering that our home-grown films are routinely subtitled and enjoyed by audiences around the world. I'm not particularly interested in what this indicates about our culture; I'm just here to write about a killer horror flick. The fact that most Americans will not see a film with subtitles is unavoidable, and it's the reason films like REC get remade, and it's also the reason why most of the people reading this have never heard of any of the films nominated for the "Best Foreign Language Film" Oscar each year.Personally, I'm glad that when watching Quarantine, I was able to focus on the terrifying images in the camera frame, instead of hurriedly trying to keep up with often-tiny text on the bottom of the screen to figure out what our heroine is screaming as she frantically scrambles up the stairs with a rampaging infected creature in tow. The POV format used here is the perfect presentation to allow us to be completely ensconced in the horror, and continuously having to shift our focus to read dialogue would disrupt that immersion, and ultimately dilute the experience this film provides. If that opinion makes me ignorant, I'm willing to accept that. But my perceived ignorance doesn't strip away the powerful gut-punch Quarantine delivers for most of its 85 minutes.The scenario here is realistic and horrifying, and the fact that a Spanish film-maker already told this story doesn't change the fact that I jumped several times during Quarantine and found myself engrossed in the the film the entire way. This is a truly scary and intense ride, and dismissing it because it previously existed in another incarnation is akin to chastising a stage production of Faust because the dialogue is translated into English. Hell, the Bible wasn't originally written in English, but I don't hear too many Christians espousing the idea that the original text was better and the modern translations used today are "rip-offs". (No, I'm not honestly comparing this film to the Bible... I'd much rather watch Quarantine than read the Bible any day). Even if American film-makers decided to take a brilliant film like Audition and recreate it for Western audiences, I wouldn't view that as blasphemy. I would just be excited that people who would otherwise never see Audition would have the opportunity to have such a twisted and original movie experience. Remakes of this ilk aren't "rip-offs", they're actually an acknowledgment that a concept is so strong that a savvy film-maker wanted that concept to be available to as large an audience as possible.It's best you don't know everything that takes place within the doomed apartment building that houses the titular Quarantine, so I won't spoil the goodness for you. But I can tell you that this film delivers many quality scares, and there is also an abundance of deliciously gruesome bits to satisfy those who enjoy the wetter side of things. Additionally, those who find themselves queasy during POV horror presentations should find the standard film-stock and often-stable camera work here a lot more palatable than the grainy, constantly-moving approach used by many of the offerings in this sub-genre.I don't know if REC is a better movie than this, but since Quarantine is such a treat, I intend to see it and find out. Ultimately, stripped of any comparisons or dismissals, this is very simply an awesome horror film. That's more than good enough for me.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<shortDescription>Realistic, intense, not for kids, frightening, this movie stays with you!</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>I walked into this movie knowing it was an American remake of a Spanish film called "REC." I walked out of the movie stunned and thinking, "Whoa." "Quarantine" is an unsettling film that is filled with what I call "that must suck" situations. For example, there is a scene in the film where a group of four people encounter an infected dog by an elevator. The elevator doors open and a drunk man staggers out, only to find that the dog is coming after him. The man rushes back into the elevator, trying desperately to close the doors before it's too late. Unfortunately for him, it was too late and the dog wound up in the sealed elevator with the man. Before the doors close, however, you can clearly see the canine tearing away at the man's face. You can also hear the sounds of crunching. Now, about the violent content. The movie is not rated R for gore, because it doesn't really need to be. It would only take about a couple more bloody deaths to earn a gore-warning in the rating. Without trying to give too much away, the usual stuff is in the movie. Infected maniacs biting at people's necks while the soon-to-be-infected scream bloody-murder. The editors did a good job adding in gruesome sound effects to go along with the gruesome scenes. However, some of the scary scenes in this film, too me, didn't quite make me jump. There was just something about the scenes that seemed like they were supposed to be scary, but not really. However, as the movie goes on, it gets more scary, gruesome, and suspenseful. The infected people have the characteristics of zombies, but they didn't come back from the dead. One of the victims trapped inside the apartment complex, a veterinarian, said that the infected people showed the same symptoms a rabid person would. However, this infection is kind of like a super-rabies. (Meaning it doesn't take long for the infection to spread.) Also, to go along with rabies, the infected have a knack for viciously killing/infecting others. These are the type that move quick, (run) like the ones in "28 Days Later." Their unearthly shrieks will send shivers down your spine. (Parents-I wouldn't recommend allowing your young children to see this movie. The result may be them crying at the utter fright that's packed into this movie.) The tone of the movie is dark. People quarantined in an apartment complex with God-knows-what scurrying around and gruesomely killing them. The cause for this unknown "outbreak" is even darker.The super of the apartment (before he gets his face ripped to shreds) said that there was a man from Boston renting the penthouse at the top of the apartment which he hadn't seen for months. When the last two survivors make their way up there looking for a way out, they encounter caged infected rats and newspaper clippings on the wall. All of the clippings said something about doomsday and doomsday cults. Also about an "Armaggedon Virus." The camera man finds a tape recorder and plays the tape in it. All you can hear sounds something like satanic chants. (Like those hidden messages when you play a record back words.) So, as the viewer, I believe that you're supposed to assume that the man from Boston was creating a doomsday virus to honor his cult.Oh, and for the record, this movie is like an exact remake of the Spanish film. (Meaning it's a scene-by-scene remake.) If you've seen "REC," in a way you've also scene "Quarantine."</fullReview>

		<date>2008-10-12</date>
		<username>sephiroth1335</username>
		<score>0.9</score>
		<shortDescription>Quarantine review</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Quarantine is an American remake of the movie Rec, a Spanish horror film released in November 2007. My initial thoughts of the movie were that it was going to be another atrocious Hollywood remake of a foreign film, such as The Ring and The Grudge. Granted, some people did enjoy the cheap scares and thrills that these remakes provided, but the overall reception of these movies within my group of peers was rather poor. This is where Quarantine differs.Quarantine is a film in first person perspective that follows a female newscaster and her cameraman on an assignment to show viewers a typical day in the L.A. Fire Department. The film starts of by setting a very calm atmosphere as the bubbly newscaster explores the firehouse and interviews firefighters. Eventually, the fire alarm sounds and she joins the firemen as they respond to a medical call within an apartment in downtown Los Angeles. The mood suddenly begins to grow more tense as we see that the call was about a person who has some sort of disease and is experiencing symptoms similar to that of rabies--the inability to swallow, partial paralysis, confusion and extreme aggression. Eventually the whole building is sealed off by the government to contain what ever disease is within. More and more people become infected and we watch as the characters of the movie fight to survive and escape the "Quarantined" building.The camera was controlled much better in this movie than it was in another first-person perspective movie that came out recently, Cloverfield. The camera work wasn't nearly as shaky as it was in Cloverfield, and people generally had a feel for what was going on at all times. The lighting was also an excellent aspect of the movie, as it seemed to parallel with the hopes of the quarantined victims.Like Cloverfield, many people are going to dislike the open ending and unanswered questions of the film. Fortunately, its much easier to piece together the origin of the virus and events preceding the movie. The movie also brings morality into question--was the government right in Quarantining the building and trapping the healthy with the infected? Is it okay to kill someone who is infected before the disease takes grasp and they lose all control, and is it okay to kill someone who has already been lost to the disease (despite their age or relation)? Another concept that I enjoyed about the movie was the makeshift weapons that were used. Unlike in many zombie-type horror movies where the main character or a supporting character has a gun with a seemingly endless supply of ammunition, the characters in this movie had to use whatever they could to fight off the infected victims.Overall, I felt the movie set a great, tense atmosphere full of scares and screams. I would highly recommend it, especially over most of the modern horror garbage that comes out (such as the latter Saw movies). If you don't mind open-ended movies or movies that take place from the first-person perspective, you won't be disappointed.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<date>2008-10-11</date>
		<username>Robert_Lovelace</username>
		<score>0.8</score>
		<shortDescription>Those Nosy News Reporters...</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>"Quarantine" is a film that, in the style of "The Blair Witch Project" (handy-cam filmed from characters' point of view), follows a news reporter named Angela Vidal (Jennifer Carpenter, of "The Exorcism of Emily Rose") who is doing a story on the job of firemen for her local news station with her cameraman, Scott, filming footage and staying the night at the fire house. Finally, a call comes in, and the group goes to an old apartment building in downtown Los Angeles, where a call came from about an elderly woman who seemed to be in trouble. Angela, Scott, and the group of the firemen get to the building, and are attacked by the old woman, who seems extremely sickly and violently aggressive. Next thing they know, the apartment complex is locked down and sealed up by the CDC, all communication blocked and everyone inside trapped with no explanation, as a bizarre infection spreads between the people. The violent and horrific events that ensue are all caught on film as the night goes on.In the past few years, these first-person handy-cam style horror movies have had their fair exercise ("The Blair Witch Project", "Cloverfield"), and "Quarantine", a remake of a Spanish film called "Rec" is the same sort of deal. The benefit to this style of film-making is that it gives it a more realistic, personal view of things, rather than a conventional form of storytelling. The beginning of the movie starts off leisurely, giving a guide through the life of a fireman by the likable Angela Vidal character, who leads us through most of the film. Then when the call comes in, things set into motion, and the real horror kicks in from there. The set-up of the entire story is interesting enough and different from the usual, which is a definite plus here. The pacing is even and not too rapid, but I will say the final thirty minutes of the movie are absolutely hysteria-ridden, and I mean that in a good way.Suspense is sustained well, and the thrills and scare scenes are really well executed, there were a couple jolts that I didn't see coming at all ("It's only bronchitis!"), and this is coming from someone who watches horror movies all the time. I also liked the fact that, naturally, it had the audience questioning the motives of the CDC and law enforcement's reasoning for quarantining the building, which become fairly clear toward the end of the movie. But the first-person experience of it made you feel like you were sort of "there" to witness all the madness and the bloodshed (the gore is also notable here, it's not too over-the-top, but the special effects were done well). The shaky camera-work adds the raw and gritty feel to it all, but is still enough for the viewers to see what is happening - they got the balance between realism and visibility right down to the wire when it came to the steady-cam. Then again, there is always the "why don't they shut the camera off?" sort of question, which is unavoidable in a first-person kind of movie like this, so it has to be forgiven if one wants to enjoy it.The acting was surprisingly superb. Jennifer Carpenter carries the movie as her character (the sweet reporter who is dying to get a good story) is emotionally and physically exhausted, by the end of the movie she is an absolute mess and she plays it extremely well. Other notable faces are Jonathan Schaech and Jay Hernandez, who both play our 'heroic' firemen. All in the all the performances from everyone were realistic and the characters felt very real. As far as the ending goes, it leaves you hanging in a way, giving little in the way of explanation of the events, but rather hinting at things for you to make up your own answers in your head. That last night vision shot of Carpenter helplessly crawling toward the camera is haunting, and that final jolt (done to death in the film's advertising) will stick with you after you've left the theater.Overall, "Quarantine" is a nice change from the norm in the horror genre. It uses an unconventional form of camera-work and storytelling, but the entire set-up is well executed and it has its fair share of scary moments. I had a lot of fun watching it, and everyone in the theater seemed to enjoy it quite a bit as well. It's an entertaining flick that provides some good thrills, although it will probably be more enjoyable if taken with less seriousness. Fun, often gruesome movie, recommended for fans of the genre. 8/10.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<username>Craig McPherson</username>
		<score>1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>Actually improves upon the original</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>In 2007 the Spanish movie REC generated an impressive buzz on the festival circuit as horror genre fan-boys, myself included, praised it as being one of the scariest movies ever made. Strong accolades, to be sure, but it was a film that pulled together elements from The Blair Witch Project, Cloverfield, 28 Days Later and the entire cinematic zombie cannon and blended them all together in a claustrophobic white-knuckler as terrifying as it was engrossing.It didn't take long before Hollywood took notice of REC's buzz, as Screen Gems scrambled to green-light an English-language remake, causing fans of the original to both bemoan Hollywood's lack of originality and predict that they would only botch the result. Which is why I'm surprised to write that not only is Quarantine every bit as good as the original, in some respects it actually surpasses it.In fact, the only area in which Quarantine failed to improve was in terms of the camera work. While both are built around the reality TV hand-held camera viewpoint, REC's cinematography was much more stable, whereas Quarantine's is so erratic as to be almost nausea inducing.Roughly 90% of this movie is a shot-for-shot remake of its predecessor, however the few deviations are key improvements over the original story, which, for the uninitiated, unfolds in a decrepit apartment building as fire department first responders, with a reality TV crew in tow, find themselves embroiled in escalating madness as a mysterious contagion begins transforming the occupants into homicidal ghouls.Writers Drew and John Erick Dowdle improve upon the original by providing the viewer with a more plausible and nuanced explanation of the disease and its inception, moving away from the macabre elements of REC in favor of a scientific basis - all of which makes Quarantine a bona-fide skin crawler, and just in time for Halloween.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<shortDescription>Good for thrills........... Too bad they don't last...........</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Quarantine is a movie that tries to push an old genre into a new direction at least for the standard American movie. While Cloverfield and the Blair Witch Project all tried to use the first person camera view for otherwise suspenseful plots, Quarantine is perhaps the best film so far to achieve something of a personal experience during a horror movie. The camera shots feel authentic and so director John Erick Dowdle should be noted for doing a good job.However, that doesn't mean there aren't problems. Quarantine is a movie that promises much and while it delivers for the first half the latter has much to be desired.Angela Vidal (Jennifer Carpenter) is a reporter covering the late shift of Los Angeles Fire Department station. With cameraman Scott Percival (Steve Harris), Angela is soon teamed with firefighters Jake (Jay Hernandez) and George Fletcher (Johnathon Schaech) who quickly show her how macho their job is. In between shots them showing off gear, the shower room, and them washing a fire truck, and the occasional bet on who can get into her Angela's pants first, Jake and Fletcher are two average firefighters who enjoy their job. Good shots but not quite the action that Angela is hoping for.Then the call comes. Angela and Scott soon jump on board Jake's and Fletcher's truck and ride off to what seems to be an average medical emergency at an apartment. An old woman, they are told, has apparently fallen and hurt herself. While not too exciting its enough for Angela to show off that something did happen.The reality of what they have walked into soon becomes apparent. The woman who they have responded for suddenly attacks a LAPD officer before being subdued. While Angela and Scott record everything with horror, Fletcher's falling body landing on the first floor lobby of the building only adds to the confusion and fear. Then, the military and the CDC show up along with a plastic covering that seals them inside with no hope of escape.Quarantine during this time introduces half dozen characters who we aren't really introduced to or even know by name. While this to be expected since Quarantine rests more on the camera's view to tell the story more than the characters, it can be frustrating in that we never really know who it is who is dying and why we should care. This becomes even more of a problem towards the end when everyone except Angela, Jake, and Scott are infected. And, what was once a clever way to introduce a story soon becomes shaking camera shots of Scott running while the three are chased by everyone up a flight of stairs until they are picked off one by one.The thrills in Quarantine are good as we try to understand what Angela, Scott, Jake, and Fletcher have walked into. The problem with Quarantine is that given the plot, and the way it is filmed, there is no real good way to end it. The deaths of everyone is expected which replaces the thrills with more horror and blood which while good is nothing special. The rumors of some kind of cult towards the end aren't really explained so we are left wondering if this was the work of one madman or some greater plan to destroy humanity. There are no real answers given which either gives more to the story or takes away depending on your point of view. The camera view can only do so much and it is unfortunate that we only have that to go on.7/10</fullReview>

		<username>Dan Grant (dan.grant@bell.ca)</username>
		<score>0.9</score>
		<shortDescription>Very creepy, very effective. One of the great horror films of 08</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Quarantine is an American remake of the movie Rec, a Spanish horror film released in November 2007. Remakes of foreign horror films are all the rage now in Hollywood. I think American writers, with the exception of Ehren Krueger and Richard Hatem (who I wish would write more) have run out of ideas so they have just left it up to foreign horror films to borrow their ideas from. You don't get a film as effectively creepy as the Ring and Blair Witch Project too often, but when you do, it should be embraced. This is one of those films. Quarantine is flat out scary.Quarantine is a film in first person perspective that follows a female newscaster and her cameraman on an assignment to show viewers a typical day in the L.A. Fire Department. The film begins rather mundanely as Angela, our intrepid reporter starts off by hanging out at the firehouse with some of the typical firefighters. One of them is Jay Hernandez, of Hostel fame. He is the "good" and "friendly" firefighter. He allows her to tag along, go places, and then when the alarm sounds, he encourages her to film everything, but stay out of the way.The mood changes abruptly once they are inside the building. What started out as a typical and ordinary call, has now turned dire as the building is sealed off, and they realize that some of the tenants of the building are experiencing what looks to be rabies type behavior--the inability to swallow, partial paralysis, extreme aggression, increased strength and mass confusion. And then the CDC shows up and begins to seal off the building. All the while, you hear sirens, helicopters and a throng of people outside the building. They will not let anyone out, including the firemen, the policemen or the camera crew. More and more people get bit and become infected with this mystery disease, and now they must fight for survival, from each other, from the military and from whatever disease has run amok in the building.The camera control in this movie was perfect. Never once did you ask yourself why they continued filming when their lives were in danger. They make a point of telling us several times that they need to film this to let the world know what is happening to them. The camera is also used as a weapon and as light in several parts of the film, all done very effectively.While the film itself, in it's entirety is very well done, the last 20 minutes is downright disturbing and incredibly scary. Without giving too much away, when the last survivors think they have found a way out, they discover a room as to where the virus might have started. And in it is what can only be described as another zombie type entity. Here they must fend for themselves and try to find their way out. You only have the light from the camera and some night vision, and it is here that Jennifer Carpenter is simply brilliant. She conveys her emotions and her fear brilliantly as she knows she is alone and to top it off, can't really see what is happening.The film raises all kinds of morality questions also. Was the government right doing what they did? Is it okay to kill someone when they haven't quite turned yet? Darwinism and questions pertaining to Darwinism are asked throughout.This is one of the most effective horror films to come out in quite sometime. This is what The Strangers could of been. This is also what many horror films aspire to be but ultimately fall short. There can be screenplay issues, an incompetent director or a combination of every deficiency possible, but refreshingly, Quarantine is not one of those films.8.5/10</fullReview>
	</review>

		<username>jzappa</username>
		<score>1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>What's a Sharp, Powerful Horror Movie Like Quarantine Doing in the Present American Market?</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Quarantine is a beacon of hope for the slasher industry. Though it may be a diamond in the ruff, it is also pure cinema. Every nuance of the film's torturous mood descending and exposition is shown through the hand-held first-person camera, but the mise-en-scene it creates is so terrifying and chaotic that we are hardly conscious of the purposefully self- conscious narrative device. The gimmick works with such pared down bare-bones impact that we are completely emotionally absorbed, and we just run with it. The reason for this impact is because it is so completely and straightforwardly subjective that all of the action is seen with the utmost possible realism, as if someone were actually there and that we may find that we've been watching not only something that one character happened to have the curious fortune to record but something that has been lost for a long time, or that may never be found. The recent Cloverfield and The Blair Witch Project have been quite penetrating in their use of cinema verite, and many have and will probably overlook this one as a cash-in on the relative audience, but it not only surpasses the tension of either of those great films but makes one very anxious to seek out its Spanish source version, Rec.The authentic visual creepiness of this both fear-externalizing and immediate mass- extermination survival-of-the-fittest fright plot is given a chilling accent by the hyper- paranoid depiction, with its post-9/11 domestic terror skulking around every assumptive corner. In spite of the impressionistic black keys, the film is unrelentingly intractible: It only wants to scare you, and it does. Without fall back on computer-generated monsters or paranormal twists or divulgences, it uses stubborn reality and escapeless claustrophobia to find new ways of being scary. Primal, often frantic, and relentless in rhythm, Quarantine is a bare-bones bloody thrill ride that should fulfill fans looking to cultivate their theatrical horror palette.Even those who passionately hate most remakes, like me for example, will have to concede that director John Erick Dowdle's jittery English-language Xerox is unabashedly frightening. If a remake must be made in place of original thoughts and ideas (I'm talking to you, Michael Bay, you cheap bastard), one could do far worse. Quarantine is the real deal, an animal, stubborn sight of primal fear and desperation rendered uninhibited. Jennifer Carpenter is realistic as the news reporter who becomes locked away in her own story, and leads an uncompromisingly realistic cast, all of whom bring an embodiment of an ultimate nightmare to this unexpected treasure in the hands of the clear technical knack of the Dowdle brothers.</fullReview>

		<fullReview>After the first few minutes, you can sort of tell that is not a high budget movie, but they still managed make a better movie than most of junk horror movies that get made.My verdict: Overall it was worth seeing. There are some well-executed surprises that will get a lot of people a little jump in their seats. After the movie, I even felt some of the ambiance while driving out of an empty parking structure at night. The story is setup as follows: A TV crew is doing a kind of "day in the life of a firefighter" report. They follow the fire team to an apartment complex where an old woman is reportedly screaming. While investigating the situation, the old building that they entered is suddenly sealed off without any reason. The camera crew captures the horrific events that begin to happen inside.Another thing that Quarantine has in its favor is a strong acting job by Jennifer Carpenter. While it's a little overdone (and likely annoying to some) towards the end, I blame that on the script. The first half of the movie demonstrates that she is interesting to watch and is immersed in her character.While I didn't quite like the ending of the movie, the ambiance (as mentioned above) did succeed in creeping me out for a good while. It even overcame my initial dislike of what seemed to be a low-budget movie. All in all, this is a worthwhile movie to see if you want to get a few good scares at the theater. For horror fans, this is a "must see" for 2008.</fullReview>
	</review>
